On wound treatment, and the quality of its material.
Wounds are made from bones, air, and pus.
And wound treatment is done in two ways. By scraping and bleeding extraction: or without scraping.
And this kind of wound treatment that is without scraping is done in two ways Or with heat Or without heat
And those wound treatments which are administered with scraping and bleeding extraction are fourteen in number throughout the body Of which one is the treatment of the Alnocrati wound, and it is the last of the head: and the Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck: and the wound treatment of the Alhagdahim, and there are two layers of the neck from two parts together: and the wound treatment of the menti is under the mandible in the lower part of the mouth: and the wound treatment of the two spatulas: and the wound treatment of the Alachosos over the end of the tail: and the wound treatment of the two focilia, and there are two of the biceps: and the wound treatment of the two cubits: and the wound treatment of the two heels.
And wound treatment does not pull out blood from small veins scattered throughout the flesh, and because of this it does not cause a loss of blood, as is the case with phlebotomy Neither is it necessary to administer wound treatment with heat, or without heat in any ailment which comes from excessive swelling, until the whole body is evacuated
Which if necessity compels us to do for wound treatment on account of an ailment, or because of custom, we administer it at every hour in the beginning of the month, at the end of the month, and in the middle of the month, and at any other time as needed
What is this, since it is from men that when it multiplies in their blood, until it requires extraction from them by means of vomiting, it finds in their head its greatest weight and burden?
And from this is that it brings about swelling and redness in their face, head, and neck. And from this is that it brings about itching in their face, and forehead, and darkness, and blindness in their eyes.
And from this is that it severs the seat of their vomiting. And from this is that their laughter multiplies. And from this is that it brings about the taste of blood in their mouth: either the tongue is swollen and expels blood. And from this is that their sleep multiplies And from this is that they urinate in their sleep blood and redness, and blood clots, and wounds, which are like these.
When therefore we see any of these things, and it is in the third middle of the month: we command vomiting to be performed immediately, after two or three hours have passed
The vomiting of the night is thus: because it itself brings about a weight in the head, and what flows down to the eyes. It is therefore necessary, that after vomiting of the whole body
These are like phlebotomies of the head
It is not lawful to use these things in those who are cold in complexion, or in whom there is catarrus For it brings about a great danger.
Neither is it proper that these should be used in the case of old men, in whom cold fevers prevail in the head.
Whoever is too assiduous above his limits will generate for himself an aversion.
And therefore it is necessary, that the surgeon should take hold of the wind, that he should place his hand with the windward side downwards, fearing lest an aversion should be generated thereby
Wind or alcohol is the place of the incision of the veins of the groin and of the veins of the belly, and because of this it confers swelling, constriction of the breath, and loss of strength, and cough, and distension of the belly
It is necessary to urge the alcohol of the wind slightly downwards, for if it is placed downwards, it generates stiffness in the heart and the stomach
The wind or alcohol, when applied too vigorously, causes pain in the head, vomiting, headaches, perforation, and toothache: and it is the place of the incision of the veins of the belly.
And it is necessary that the surgeon take hold of the wind, that he should not place his hand with the incision: that he may not cut the artery and cause the flow of blood to be obstructed.
The alcohol of the wind under the chin causes swelling in the mouth, corruption of the gums, and similar diseases which are in the mouth: and it is the place of the incision of the veins which are in the two lips.
The alcohol of the wind causes spitting, which is from reflection and heat.
The alcohol of the wind inside the two cavities of the cheeks causes the drawing out of the veins of the groin, of the veins of the belly, and of the veins of the head: for it attracts blood from all these small veins which are in the flesh: and it attracts these small veins from other large veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
It is necessary, therefore, that the surgeon take heed, not to advance too far in the incisions: for in such a place the flesh is stripped bare, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
The winds of the intestines cause swelling over the ulcers of the rectum, and lower ulcers.
It is necessary, therefore, that the surgeon take heed, so that the incisions be large and open to the air: for in such a place the veins are liable to be broken by excessive suction.
The veins of the limbs grow thin from diminished circulation: for they draw blood from all over the body, and cause ancient pains in the buttocks, thighs, and calves: they cause menstruation, and bring about swelling, and pain: and are in the place of two saphenous veins, and the marrow bones.
Indeed, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many people.
The marrow bones are very near to the veins of the limbs.
The position of the veins is such that you should apply the incision to the first wound and moderate the suction: and do not prolong the position of the incision, but place it easily and remove it easily, so that the humors may rise to their proper place: and do not desist from this process until the area is red and swollen, and the blood appears red: then indeed you may perform the incision, and slowly resume the suction.</start_of_turn> Consider also the position of the body.
For one who has little flesh and few hairs, it is necessary to make the incision once, not twice: so as not to tear the place.</start_of_turn>
And advise the Wind-Surgeon, that he increase the scarification, and make it shallow: and temper the suction to facilitate, and speed, and the movement of the instrument.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary, that he make the incisions twice In the first incision he opens a small opening for the evacuation of blood, and washes it: and in the second incision he makes an exquisite extraction of the thick blood
If the blood is dark green, then make a third incision: in order to obtain the utmost
Finally, when we wish to extract the blood slowly, or little by little, sufficeth us one incision. When we wish to extract a great quantity of blood, we make many incisions.
And if we measure that the blood is thick: then it is necessary, that he make a deep incision: and the equal endpoint of the incision is the depth of the skin.
What must be administered from the oils at the position of the Wind-Surgeon, and what is the cause of the wind-surgeon?
In that which the skin is thick, hard, rough, and has close pores, it is necessary that you anoint the area of the Wind-Surgeon with open, soothing, dissolving oils: if it is in summer, such as almond oil, or oil of violets, or sweet fennel oil, or cucumber seed oil
And if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or camomilla oil, or salve: and the like.
If there is excessive coldness, the oil must be amaranth oil, or balsam oil or white oil, or almond oil: and the like.
If the belly is full of wind, hold meat: then it is necessary to prevent the bile from going out, and it is necessary to wash the places of the winds after the letting of wind with rose-water, or with cold water, or with salt water, or with cucumber water, or with rainwater: and the like.
If the blood of a person is full of moisture, wash the winds after letting with vinegar, or with myrtle water, or with alum: and the like.
If there are many thick humours in the blood, wash the winds after letting with old wine, or with rue water, or with fennel decoction, or chamomile: and like these things.
It is necessary to bathe in a bath, or in a bathhouse, immediately after the letting of the wind, and it is necessary to take something for an hour, or two hours, after coming out of the bath.
And it is not necessary for anyone to sleep after the letting of the wind.
What is it that is necessary to do in order to regulate the wind, or to do phlebotomy before the letting of the wind?
And after this it is necessary to consider: then if the wind is choleric, or if phlebotomy is necessary, and if there is an excess of heat in the blood: then it is necessary to take something cooling, such as pomegranate seed: and also barley with vinegar and lettuce, and chicory, and mugwort: and like these things.
And put the meat on a low diet, and eat coarse bread, and feed the boiled things with fennel: and like these things.
And as for one who has a cold complexion, it is necessary to take in a drink of honey syrup, or of aloes, or of acacia honey: and drink a moderate wine, which is between new and old: and it is recommended to eat little food And place its meat on the cocks, and dogs: and birds, that is, sparrows: and the meat of pigeons.
And it is necessary that the liquid of the diet on the day of vomiting and bleeding should be more than the food: and it is necessary that it be given in a liquid in which some man has eaten in the hours of twilight or before vomiting, or medicine of the muscle, that is, muskroot, or alehoof before vomiting, or after: in order to comfort the principal members and to temper the blood. And it is not permitted to take warm food in the liquid.
Of Vomiting without Incision. But those vomiting which are without incision are those which arise from the excess of food in the upper parts of the body, and the spleen, and the foot, and over the belly, the navel, and the region of the kidneys, and the piece of the intestine of the appendix: because these members do not suffer incision over themselves
And it is not necessary to seek for its ease, except for an attack from a member of the blood to a member: as we place Vomiting on the nipples in the flux of blood from the veins. And we must take care that the cold of the vomiting does not injure the adjacent member: as we place vomiting on the belly and the navel For indeed it dilates the member, and warms it, and removes the pain thereby, because it resolves the said vomiting.
It is also placed over the kidneys, when it happens in the eating of cheese, or bread For its own attraction is perhaps opening the vomiting, or driving out the stone from its place.
And in the same way it does when it is placed over the spleen and the upper part of the stomach in the vomiting of both of them.
These winds are administered with a small incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered with warm water in pleuritic ailments: and this is when the Wind is to be applied, and be great, only warm water alone, or water in which certain herbs have been boiled together.</start_of_turn>
The form of Wind administered with fire.</start_of_turn>
The breadth of its opening is two fingers wide, according as we form it: and the quantity of it in depth is half a palm, and its breadth is in the middle of it, a small hole, according to the quantity of the needle which is to be inserted: it is made of air, or citrine, thick at the edges, soft, even, rough. Let it not harm the limb in its position over itself: and there shall be in its middle a transverse bar of iron or steel, on which is set the candle with fire.</start_of_turn
And thus is this wind made great, greater than we have narrated, and somewhat less than that, according to the ailments and the age of the patient who uses it.</start_of_turn
The wind of children is great, and those of the extenuates are other than those of men, and of the gross.</start_of_turn
The quality of placing this wind with fire upon the limb is that you incline the wick with fire from the center outwards, or put a candle already alight from pitch: and put it upon the middle of the transverse bar which is in the middle of the wind, so that the fire is surrounded below the lower part of the wind, so that it does not burn the patient.</start_of_turn
Then place it upon the limb: and the finger rests upon the hole which I mentioned, until we have finished with the wind: when we have finished, we remove the finger, and the vapor escapes from that hole, and the wind is at rest immediately.</start_of_turn
Then give the purgative according to its manner, and repeat if it does not work.
In cases of intestinal obstruction due to wind, which is administered with water, there is neither a transverse nor a circular bougie: it is not administered unless the abdomen is filled with water, and is placed over the limb only.
This is its form.
The larger the dose of this purgative, the more water it will absorb
On the suspension of blood-sugar.
Blood-sugars are not administered in multiple places, except in those places where the position of the intestines is not possible due to their density, such as the lip and the tongue, and things of a similar nature Or in a limb stripped of flesh, such as the finger and the nose, and things of a similar nature
The quality of the purgative is: that it be made from blood-sugars which are in sweet water, freed from putrefactions
Then they are given every day and night in sweet water, until the patient is relieved of hunger, and nothing remains in their belly Then let the body be evacuated at first with a phlebotomy, and with purges. Then strip the limb of the patient, until it turns red Then place over itself.
When they have been administered, and have gone down, and the place has been sucked with the purge, then it is possible to suck the place with the vomit: and if not, then evacuate the place with vinegar Then, when there is much water, and the wound is raw, and has burst open.
When the flow of blood persists after a burst blood vessel, and there is oozing: then pour a piece of linen cloth into cold water, and place it over the wound, until the oozing stops.
When there is bleeding profusely, then sprinkle powdered alum or gall, and similar things like sponges from the sponges: until the bleeding stops.
Or place over the wound cloths of linen which have been scraped: and let them go, until the cloth sticks to the place, for the blood will stop bleeding.
And it is necessary, if possible, to change the dressing of the blood vessel repeatedly.
If the bleeding is forbidden from being suspended, then the place is washed out with blood which has recently escaped Or hold pressure in the place, until something comes out of the blood Then put on [pressure], for when they feel something coming out of the blood, they adhere immediately.
When therefore they wish to fall asleep, sprinkle upon them something from aloe which has been pounded, or salt, or ashes: and they will fall asleep immediately.
This chapter is also very useful in the art of Medicine, and is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of bones.</start_of_turn
Hear ye, sons of learning! For they that handle this book of the art of healing and of wondrous remedies, who have never once perused the works of the ancients, neither learned a single letter from them.
And thus it is that this method is hidden from our land's sciences I have learned nothing good from it in this matter, and I am not skilled in it except in so far as I have learned from the great books of the ancients, and from their wisdom.
Therefore I have clung to experience and the length of my life.
I have set down for you in this chapter all the science which I have learned: and that which has passed my experience, after I had abridged it for you, and I have cast off that which was not essential, and have abridged that which remained, and have brought it to a close. And I have devised many forms of instruments which are used in this work, with the addition of their declension, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
**Chapter II.**
It is necessary, as we have said in the prologue to this chapter, to recall the main points and chapters which are to be considered in the first place by the artisan in his progress: that is to say, those who are learned and those who are prompt, should know, or learn, or add to this noble art, or others from you.
I say then, when any fracture, dislocation, twisting, or falling occurs: then it is necessary immediately to draw blood from him, and to bind up the wound, or both together: if nothing else hinders, or is lacking, such as weakness of spirit: or that the patient is young or old and decrepit: or that the time is urgent, either because of the heat or the cold.
Then he shall be satisfied with his food, with cold herbs, and with birdsâ€™ flesh, and with blood-vessels: and he shall abstain from strong drink, and from fatty meats, and shall restore himself from food and from all food which fills the veins. When he is secure from the heat of the after-flux, and does not fear effusion of the matter to the limb: then the patient may return to his ordinary regimen as soon as he is accustomed to it.
When the bone is begun to be restored, then it is necessary, that the patient be nourished with nourishing and bulky food, such as barley bread boiled with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and entrails of sheep, and fish, and fresh eggs: and strong drink, and things similar to these With this regimen the coagulation of the fracture is quicker and better
And thou knowest, that bones which are broken when they are in the joints which are in the prime of life, and are decrepit, are not able to continue and consolidate according to their natural tendency always, because of the drying up of the members: and they consolidate and continue only as much as the parts which are in the end of softness, as for example bones of the limbs of new-born children.
But nature annexes to the broken bone from every side something like glue, which has thickness, by which it adheres and contracts it, until they are consolidated together: and they are bound together until they come to the end of their strength and firmness, as it was in the beginning, until it is forbidden by its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you give the patient nourishing food which has thickness, and weight, and coarseness, as we have said
And thou knowest, that there are various kinds of fractures, according to the diversity of the members: for example, a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the skull: and a fracture of the breastbone is different from a fracture of the backbone: and in the same way all the other limbs are different from each other: and I come with the remembrance of the kind of fracture which is exposed in his chapter distinguished from the others. Diverse forms of bone fractures also exist within themselves.
Since it is arched in itself, a fracture may also happen in the middle of the bone due to these stars.</start_of_turn> When a fracture occurs along the entire length of the bone, such fractures are called satagiae, that is, stars, and are like scratches made by a knife.</start_of_turn> These fractures are not caused by blows or wounds but by a small crack in the head of the bone.</start_of_turn
Every kind of fracture has its own natural ability, or an instrument peculiar to its restoration, according to the memory of the fracture, distinguished in its place.</start_of_turn
From this it is known that a bone fracture is characterized by its crookedness, its prominence, and its sensation and sound when the limb is compressed.</start_of_turn
When, however, there is no apparent crookedness, no sound under your touch when you touch the bone with movement, and no great pain is found: then it is not a fracture, but perhaps only a slight bending or a small crack. In such a case it is not necessary to move the limb with the hand and compress it entirely, but rather to place above it what the physician remembers, after which the tension should be gradually restored.</start_of_turn
You know that when a bone is arched and breaks into two, besides the stars, all the parts are loosened from their neighbours. In such a case it is necessary for you to begin from the very beginning to restore it, equalizing it before any swelling appears.</start_of_turn
If however swelling appears, leave it for a few days until the swelling subsides.</start_of_turn Then it is equal to itself according to whatever mode it is possible from lenity and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration of it and its equality is easier than bones in which fractures have occurred.</start_of_turn>
Then bind it up according to what you remember of its reminder.</start_of_turn>
If indeed there are fractures in the bones, then it is necessary to extend this fractured limb from two parts, if it is the hand, or the foot: or with the hand, if it is a small limb: or with two bands, or with a band and a hand.</start_of_turn
And let your position be such as you place the limb in a place equal to itself, according to its natural form, until the parts of the fractured limb are extended: then endeavor to make them return to their original positions with all ingenuity, and according to whatever mode you can in the places: and be careful according to your strength, lest you cause more pain or damage through your operation, and endeavor with your strength so that you can apply one bone with its fellow, according to better forms.</start_of_turn>
And it is necessary in this hour to try and touch your hand.</start_of_turn If there is anything diverse there, rectify it, and make it equal to that quantity which is in your power.</start_of_turn
And be careful of excessive extension and compression, as do many surgeons.</start_of_turn For many of them cause this to happen through their operation, either by making the swelling greater or by causing contraction in the limb: as we have seen this to happen through their operations many times.</start_of_turn
Then bind up after the equality, and loosen the operation, and tighten the limb, and rest, and tranquility: and give the patient rest over it.</start_of_turn And make the patient recline, lest he move in the hour of his greatest heat, and his sleep during the course of his revolution, and his agitation, and at egestion, and all his movements in the end shall be in his weakness. And that he recline, lest his limb remain in a position which follows pain For when he feels pain in a position of the limb, or an injury: then it is necessary to change it to another position from which he does not feel pain.
And that his position be equal and straight: lest there arise stiffness in the limb when restoring it
The degree of stiffness of the fractured limb is according to what is narrated.
You know that fractured limbs are diverse in their parts, size, and shape
For those which are small, such as the arm, the finger, the heel, and the like, it is necessary to apply soft padding, fine But for those which are large, such as the thigh and the leg, it is necessary to apply a broad and hard padding
Because the broad ligament connects the great limb and stretches it from all parts with equal force, so that it does not adhere to the wound
Then, after you have evacuated, start to apply a plaster that is convenient for this, on the stump of the fracture itself. Then, start to bandage the ligament over the fracture site three or four times, according as is necessary for the limb: and keep your hand firm with the ligament.
Then, support the part above the fracture site from the fracture site and bind less than you bound the fracture site. Then extend the limb with a gentle inward curve at the fractured part, and soften the tightening gradually until you can remove the diseased part from the sound tissue.
Then take hold of the limb once more and curve it inwards once more over the fractured area with inward curves.</start_of_turn> Then support it with an inward curve towards the lower part of the fracture.</start_of_turn> And let your operation be in the tightening of the curve and the softening of its tension, as we said in the first inward curve.</start_of_turn
Then place between the inward curves a soft plug of cotton or cloth, so that it equals the thickness of the fracture, if there is such thickness: and if not, do not place anything in it.</start_of_turn
Then curve the limb over once more.</start_of_turn> Then make the two inward curves level with each other, and hold them steady, if there is no swelling, neither a stiff abscess.</start_of_turn
If, however, there is swelling or a stiff abscess in that place: then place over that what sits on that abscess, and drain the swelling: and leave it alone for a few days.</start_of_turn Then bind over it once more, and keep the asclepiads in place.</start_of_turn
And let the bands be made from the middle of broad and strong reeds, or from wood from which cribri are made, or from palm branches, or from flax stems: and let the width of every bandage be according to this shape and this figure.</start_of_turn
It is true that the bandage which is put over the limb should be wider and thicker than the rest of the bandages: and the length of the bandage should be according to the size and shape of the limb.</start_of_turn
Then string over Astellas with the other, according to the first stricture. Then bind over with strings as before, with the centre towards the outside, as we have said concerning the stricture: and it is that the stricture should be greater over the site of the fracture: and the greater the distance from the fracture, the smaller the stricture.
It is necessary that the strings be of medium size and flexibility, and that they be made of soft linen.
For if the strings are large, like the ropes of stone masons, it is a great error, because the stricture will fall with them outside the measure
And fine strings are again unsuitable: for you will not follow the stricture with them as with those things which you wish to follow.
And it must not be less between the Astellas than the breadth of a finger
Which if the sick man is pained beyond the ends of the Astellas after the stricture in healthy places: then put under the skin a cushion of soft wool, or carded lanolin, until nothing is pained from that.
When there is a fracture with a wound, and it happens that the tissue is ruptured in the skin, I shall come with a reminder of that in the second chapter concerning the singularity.
And you know that not every fractured limb should be strapped with Astellas from the first day. For when the limb is great, it is not necessary to bind over with Astellas immediately, but after five or seven days, or more, according to your security concerning the progress of the wound
And this is the way of dressing wounds, which the ancient people used to restore, which they put over fracture and dislocation.
The common abbreviated plaster, restoring the fracture, is suitable for various complexions, especially women and children: since it neither increases nor decreases heat.
Take from the flour of the mill, and the barley meal which hangs upon the sides of the millstone when the mill is in motion: and make of these things as before, except that you must not make it too thick, nor too thin. Use it then.
Another method of plastering, restoring the fracture, dislocation and torsion Rice flour, laudanum, acacia, enula and mugath: and vinegar, in total ten myrrh, and aloes two and a half, and alethil twenty, mix them all together, and crush, and mix with lukewarm water or with gypsum if the patient's complexion is hot. Use this plastering. For it is a good composition, restores the fractured bones quickly, and is suitable for many people.
Another plastering method that also helps with fractures and torsion Mugath, flour, white alum, in total ten myrrh, and aloes two and a half, acacia six bol of Armenian bole and twenty, crush them all together, mix with water, or with gypsum. Administer immediately
Method of plastering for bone joints that are separated from their places: and it soothes the pain of the accident, and closes the fracture of the bone and the break of the bone Take fine linen, soak it in vinegar and decocted oil: and put it on the place
Neither is there any restoration to be had in this Emboplastrum, but it is good to apply a warm poultice: and it drives away the pain itself.
The proper treatment for the Embplasia is to restore the fractured bone. Take broad leaves of fig and rough leaves of willow: and urine them together: and make an embolus from both.
The brief treatment of the embolus is to apply it to the fracture of the bone: and when you desire the resolution of the remaining apostematis Recipe for the root of angelica, chamomile, violets and barley flour, take all together: mix them with wine, if there is no warm limb.
If there is indeed a warm limb, then mix it with humid coriander water, or with water, and use it like that
Another method of treatment for the embolus is stronger for the resolution than the first one, when there is a hard apostemata during the restoration of the limb Recipe for the root of angelica, flaxseed, barley, mastic, sambuca, violets and chamomile, take all together: mix them with salty water, or sweet water, or wine This whole thing according to the warmth of the limb, and the quietness of its heat.
Hippocrates indeed did not speak in his book about placing anything upon the fractured limb during its restoration, except for butter made from cheese: nothing else: and he said that there should be a middle ground between the thickness and thinness
Galen, however, considers it useful to place upon the fractured limb during its restoration various substances that contain the exication of heat, such as myrrh, aloes and olibanum, and the like of these things which we have mentioned
Method of Embasting which pertains to the wound and the pain. Method of Embasting which pertains to the wound and the pain. Take parts of mugwort, ciceris, human hair cut, or feathers of birds, and barley and salt in equal quantities: mix them together, crush and form them: make from this the embastment.
As for the quantity, however, according to the need, that the ligature remain, afterwards dissolve it, that is to say, as you consider, if not at once, that no pain, nor itching, nor movement of the fractured bone from its place occur: then do not dissolve it for many days. If in its place there occur itching or tingling, or painful swelling: then act promptly, and dissolve it in an hour, and remove the embastment from it: and take a soft cushion, or a sponge of marine wool, wet: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and bathe with it until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides.
If in its place there occur itching or tingling, or painful swelling: then act promptly, and dissolve it in an hour, and remove the embastment from it: and take a soft cushion, or a sponge of marine wool, wet: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and bathe with it until the itching ceases, and the pain subsides. Then let the limb rest for an hour. Then place upon it a dry cloth, immersed in vinegar and olive oil, or olive oil of roses: and bind over it at night once, until you are secure from a hot after-burn, and the swelling of the limb has subsided, and the pain has ceased.
Then repeat it with slight tightening, and a small embastment: and do not tighten it too tightly at first, but gradually with it.
If you have removed the cause, the swelling, the redness, and the pain, and all the other symptoms have disappeared, and the embastment and the tightening are unnecessary, then repeat it as you did at the beginning equally.
If nothing further happens to the limb of which we have spoken, do not dissolve it, except after three days, or four, or five, or seven days When the wound has healed for forty days in total, according to what appears from the solution of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture has healed and coagulation of the flesh has approached above: then add further strengthening than the first throughout: and also add in the digestion of nourishing food, as has been remembered.
If however the place of the fracture has already begun to abate and expand more than is necessary: then you know that nutrition is hindered from reaching it You must therefore bathe it with warm water at every time you wash it, and keep it so for three days, and reduce the dressing somewhat.
By this means the limb will receive nutrition and will heal more quickly.
That which the rest of the restorers of fractures do carelessly with regard to the limb, when they do not restore it at first as they should, according to what is necessary, and restore it crookedly: this is an error in their operation, and a great fear: for if it had been right, the old writers would have said so in their books, and they would have operated in this way.
I have never seen anyone do this in this way, and it is better to do it right, so that it does not go wrong.
Of the dressing of an injury to the head.
There are various kinds of fractures, and their figures are many: and their causes are diverse.
For a fracture is that which is caused by a blow with a weapon: and the blow raises the whole bone until it reaches the marrow, which is under the bone, as an axe does to wood: and for this reason this kind of fracture is called Axial
If any bone is fractured, and if its face is fractured, and the incision does not penetrate to its very end: this kind of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, absolute fracture.
A fracture is great or small.</start_of_turn>
A fracture is either a break or a crushing: the cause of which is impact with a stone, or falling from a height, or something similar.</start_of_turn>
There are also penetrating fractures, which are those which approach the bone substance, either on the surface of the bone or in its substance: these are great or small fractures.</start_of_turn
There is also an occult fracture which lies within the substance of the bone beneath the hair: this is a small fracture, and for this reason it is called the capillary fracture.</start_of_turn>
A fracture is caused by a fall, or by impact with a stone, or by something similar, which produces a bending of the bone inwards: as when a gust of wind breaks a bowl when struck.</start_of_turn
This mostly happens in the heads of wet bones, as in the heads of children.</start_of_turn>
And all these kinds of fractures are separated by a swelling which I shall remind you of in their proper place.</start_of_turn>
We recognize all these kinds of fractures by their appearance on the surface, by their tenderness, and by the excision of the corrupted flesh which is above the wound.</start_of_turn
The capillary fracture is recognized by the fact that it is seen on the bone, is stripped away, and is bound above with resin: the fracture itself appears black.</start_of_turn

On the restoration of a fractured arm
The arm is composed of two bones, which are named the two ulnae.
One of them is the forearm: and that which follows the thumb And the other is the upper arm, and that which is placed under the forearm from the lower part
Perhaps therefore the greater bone is fractured alone, or the forearm: and perhaps both are fractured together
When therefore the fracture of the upper forearm occurs, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier
And when the lower bone is fractured, the fracture is bad, and the healing is difficult
And it is worse when two bones break together.
If indeed one bone which has broken is slightly above the middle, then the physician must be consulted in order to restore it, so that he may put back the fragment with ease, until it is equal again.</start_of_turn>

It is necessary to put on the figure of the hand at the restoration of it, and to extend the limb beyond the wrist.</start_of_turn> And let the thumb of the hand be above the height of all the fingers: and let the lower joint be above all the fingers, or the rest: and let the limb be sitting square upon itself, and let the wrist be forward of it, or in a straight line, in height, so that the limb does not incur any strain.</start_of_turn>
Then extend the limb of the arm from the lower part, or the hand itself, or with the ligament.</start_of_turn And the other limb extends in like manner.</start_of_turn>
Then the physician must equal the bones, until he has reduced them to a better shape than before.</start_of_turn
And if there are gaps in the fracture of the bone, then try to reduce all the fragments to their place with your care.</start_of_turn
And if indeed there appears in it a separated fragment, and it has burst through the skin, and there is no hope of restoring it: then find above it, and remove it according to the manner which we mentioned earlier.</start_of_turn
And if indeed the fracture is with a wound, I will make for that the single head, so take care of the cure of that from here on.</start_of_turn
If in the beginning of your restoration the heel is stiff, then stretch the band with a slip made from oleoso resin, white lead, and be equal between the thickness and fineness: and bind the band above the stricture with a fine string, until the heel settles down: then remove the slip and apply a plaster prepared from pounded barley flour with eggs. Then apply sponges, and let the sponge which is placed over the fracture be wider and stronger
You know that the number of sponges of the Brachium is six in various positions: if the fracture is in one socket, or in two sockets together.

And let there be cloths which cover the fracture, soft cloths, wet: and not hard, rough And let the thread which is used to tie be from flax, medium between fine and coarse, as we have said.
And consider the limb and the ligament after days. If anything happens whose rectification is necessary, such as itching which occurs in the limb: then it is necessary to bathe the limb with warm water, until the itching ceases, and then loosen the limb, until it is quiet, and then repeat the bandage.
If the bandage is now softened, and the bone is now removed, and things are like these: then rectify all this with your skill, and consider again.
For if the nourishment is hindered from reaching the limb, because of the excess of the bandage, then it is necessary to soften it somewhat, and remove it for a few days, until the nourishment comes to it again, and then tie it again
If, however, no improvement occurs in the patient with anything we have mentioned, it is not necessary to persist in the treatment, except after twenty days or thereabouts.
The treatment of the patient is to be suspended for a period of twenty days or thereabouts. The patient's condition should be stable, and any disturbing factors should be avoided. Let him sleep upon his back
Know that this fracture heals in thirty days, or in thirty-two days; and sometimes in eighty days This is all in accordance with the temperaments of the complexion and the virtue of the patient.
Regarding the restoration of fractures of the hand
Know that fractures of the bones of the hand and the joints of the fingers are rare They do not occur except with great attrition

And if the fracture be of the remaining fingers, as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then it must be equalised, and bound with the healthy finger which follows it: or they may be bound all together according to the continuation: and that is better Or place upon it a star which is firm as we said concerning the thumb.
And consider in the restoration of it, and after the setting of the limb: and oppose with it that which is required, when anything of that happens again, according as its narration is repeated.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the thigh bone.

On the restoration of a fractured limb of the shin.
You know that the shin consists of two bones, of which one is thick and is called the Cruris, and the other is thin and is called the Focile.
Just as happens with both kinds of fracture that occur in the bones of the forearm: and because of this, its restoration is equal to that of the forearm, and the operation is the same
If both bones are fractured together, the shin turns towards all parts
If the thin bone is fractured, the shin turns towards the anterior part
If the thick bone is fractured, it is found beneath: and this is from that which does not cover the bone

But it is necessary to know that if the fracture of the shin is a compound fracture, having much swelling: so that the extension should be less, and the alleviation more gentle

As for the restoration of fractures of bones, when they are associated with a wound.</start_of_turn
It agrees with him upon whom it has happened that a fracture is associated with a wound, especially if it is great, such as the hip bone, or the thigh bone, or the forearm, so that you must immediately perform phlebotomy: if you recognize that there are conditions for phlebotomy, such as the above.</start_of_turn
If there is bleeding from the wound, then it is necessary to order the abscission of it, so that it is powdered above the wound: if there is nothing else present.</start_of_turn
Then begin to restore the fracture on the same day: and do not postpone it, if it does not occur spontaneously.</start_of_turn
If indeed spontaneous healing occurs, then cease the restoration on the ninth day, until the swelling has subsided.</start_of_turn And do not approach it on the third day, and the fourth day altogether, for you will cause him to suffer additional injuries.</start_of_turn
If indeed the fractured bone is prominent above the skin detected: then it is necessary to order that you reduce it, and equalize it with your hands with ease and with little extension.</start_of_turn>
If, however, it is not prepared for extension, and its equality is equal to your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. The instrument is made of iron, its length being equal to the quantity of seven fingers, or eight: and its width according to the quantity of the wound. Therefore, the physician must take three, or four according to the quantity that is necessary for the cure of every kind of fracture
It should be rounded: and its size should be small, so that it does not double under compression in the hour of operation: and its edges should be acute, which should have a margin on their edges. And its upper part should reach from the middle to the lower parts.
This is its shape
It is carried out in the operation, so that its acute edge is placed curved over the protruding extremity of the bone: and it is pushed in with that liquid one until the bone returns and is equal to four times its equality.
To attempt to make the extremities of the fracture equal to each other on one side.
If its extremities are slightly fractured and do not grasp well with the instrument, then it is permissible to cut off the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power over it
If, however, it does not seem possible to reduce them with this as we have said throughout: then cut it with that which corresponds to the Scissors which we mentioned: or bind it with one clamp in whatever way is possible to you
Then leave that which remains in the bone from asperities and small cortices.
When the bone has been reduced, and it is found to be painful after reduction, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural position
Do this then. Do this then. For you confer great relief to the sick when you complete your restoration of them as much as you do the bone.
Then immerse the bandage in strong vinegar, black. And if it is in summer. And do not place upon the wound a greasy cover or anything containing oil: so that it does not cause it to become inflamed and corrupt
Then administer as necessary at the end of your restoration of the bone And remove the wound: thus, you will cut through with scissors the hole in the swelling according to the quantity of the wound
And take care in all things, lest you tighten the wound with the fracture. Many times, indeed, have these things been done carelessly by doctors: and they have caused accidents to their patients, either death or corruption, or swelling, that is, illness. And let your treatment be gentle, lenient, diverse in the strictures of the remaining fractures
If the wound is bad, or if it is large, and you fear some other mishap which we have mentioned, and if you find pain in the suspended part: then it is not proper to put on any plaster. And make for yourself from strong paper incisions in the place of the plaster, and tighten it with these.
When however, after one or two days, and the wound has begun to heal, pus has formed: then remove from above the plaster which you put on with the vinegar Then administer ointment and unguents which are customary with us for healing wounds: such as ointment of butter and the like
And it is necessary for you to solve the ligament. Consider the wound every morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed.</start_of_turn>
It is necessary to prepare the limb in such a way that the pus may drain downwards easily.</start_of_turn>

On the treatment of the swelling caused by a fractured bone.
Many times this swelling occurs in the area of healing of a fractured bone. Specifically, which is close to the joint: and it is caused by the shape of the limb And sometimes it hinders the limb from its natural action
Therefore consider at once, if the swelling is recent: administer in it medicines which have styptic properties, such as aloe, and olibanum, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and the like.
Yes, take some of these from these: and mix them with wine which has styptic properties, or with albumin or with vinegar: and apply them over the swelling on a cushion, and bind them over the fracture itself securely
And remove the support, and do not keep it bound for many days. Then loosen the limb, and repeat this until the swelling subsides
Or bind over the limb a layer of lead foil which has been softened in the middle Lead has the property of removing all that is threatening to the limbs
If however the swelling has already hardened and is fixed, and there is need to remove it: then find above the limb from its upper part, and cut away the excess: or scrape the limb with some rough objects until the swelling subsides: and take care of the limb until it heals
When the limb is restored and the swelling has subsided, the limb remains under its natural shape
When the fracture of a bone has been restored, and the limb remains below it subtle and feeble, it does not become well except for multiple causes. When the fracture of a bone has been restored, and the limb remains below it subtle and feeble, it does not become well except for multiple causes.
When the fracture of a bone has been restored, and the limb remains below it subtle and feeble, it does not become well except for multiple causes, such as the stiffness of the ligament, which hinders the nourishment of the limb, or excessive embrocation at an inappropriate time, or for want of blood in the body and its weakness. And bathe the limb. And give it comfort and joy over it.
Then put a plaster on the limb: so that it attracts plenty of nourishment to it. And anoint it regularly with warm water, until the nourishment recovers and returns to its natural shape.
Â¶ On the treatment of fractures when they have been restored tortious.
When the limb has been restored after healing from its fracture, and its bending persists, or there is a swelling: and the limb is hindered by that shape, although the limb is not prohibited from its natural action by the operation, it does not follow that it should be broken again
And there have been many proud doctors and restorers in our land who have done this.
And this operation is very dangerous, harmful, leading to death, or great fear, as it is mortal.
But if the limping and swelling are recent: then it is necessary to anoint it with warm water containing soothing herbs, such as elder leaves, and the root of this, and melilot, and similar things And let there be made for him a patch with softening plaster, as for a diachronic centre made.
Or let there be taken root mucilage of elderberry and pounded with fat of a hen, or with beeswax: and plastered thereupon.
Or let there be taken poppy seed, and ground with columbine seed, and such like things as are named by the physicians to reduce consolidation
And when the swelling has been softened by constant friction, as of old, and with moist hands. And move the limb about to every part in every hour.
But if the swelling has preceded the pain, and has become strong, and has become fixed in stone, and has required excision, then it is necessary to cut off the excess above it, and to dissolve the continuation of the bone, and to remove what remains of the swelling or bone with fine instruments: and facilitate this with request and care
From the discourse on Dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any articulation from its place. Because it hinders its movement and deforms the limb's figure And causes the production of greater pain, and wounds which are dangerous to the limb.
When therefore such a dislocation has happened, then it is necessary to act with speed to restore it: and do not delay at all
For if it is delayed, the place will become swollen, and perhaps with this the reduction of the dislocation will be more difficult
Therefore it is not necessary to move or extend it during the swelling Since many things cause spasms and painful aches.
When such a thing happens, it is necessary to perform phlebotomy on the patient immediately. Then let it be discharged until the swelling subsides
And it should be bound with warm water and oil Let the swelling be gently reduced
And care for every limb so that its memory remains in its place
I command you to join the head of dislocation and the extremities of the fracture from the upper part of the body to the lower part of the body
As for the care of the lower jaw dislocation
The two mandibles rarely dislocate except in rare cases.
And both dislocations happen according to two different methods. Either by slight displacement from their places and gentle softening. Or by complete dislocation, lasting until they are softened at the breast, until the patient can no longer bear the pain, and cannot close his mouth, and his tongue is swollen with saliva
When the dislocation is slight, it returns by itself without any treatment
If, however, the dislocation is complete, lasting: it is necessary to perform a reduction of it immediately, and it must not be delayed: and let the physician keep hold of the thumb of his hand with one hand on the root of the dislocation of the mandible between the bones, if the dislocation is from one side only If the dislocation is from two parts, let him introduce two fingers simultaneously. The remaining fingers of his hand should be outside, so that they may support it equally.
Furthermore, he must command the patient to open his jaw widely and grasp with all his teeth The doctor must stabilize the jaw and urge the jaw back to its place until it returns to its normal position
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are displaced simultaneously: then he must give him a lukewarm drink of water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy: neither must he attempt to reduce them both at once, as we have said before
When they have returned and are equal, and the patient's bone is closed, and they are not softened: then he must place over the teeth of the patient two fingers, with wax which is made of beeswax: and oil of roses.
And then they are bound with soft ligaments.
The patient should sleep on his back: and his head should be resting between two cushions, so that he does not move it to the right or left: neither must he be forced or urged to swallow: rather let him eat slowly, until when he has moved the pain, and closed the jaw: then he may eat what he wants, and this should be given to him with ease.
He should not toil over the operation of his mouth during eating and drinking, and swallowing, until the jaw is closed and healed
If the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time, and they are not returned to their places: then many times fever and bitter vomit follow: and perhaps the patient's belly becomes soft, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
When therefore the aid has been performed: then you know that it is a loss, and many times the patient dies from it within ten days.
Regarding the reduction of dislocation of the Furcula and the extremity of the humerus
The Furcula indeed does not dislocate from the inside outwards, because of its continuity with the chest It dislocates outwards sometimes: and it is declared by the sense.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back, and extends his arms Then cover the place with your palm of your hand with a strong bandage, for it will return by itself
Then place over it a pad, and beads, and bind it tightly
The extremities following the elbow do not dislocate with it, except rarely If indeed it dislocates on some day: then it is necessary that it be reduced and equalised as we have said, and that it be prepared for you
Then place over it a pad, and beads, and bind it tightly And command the patient to adhere to tranquility and quiet, until he is healed
And when the same cure has reduced the extremity of the Humerus, when it is also separated from its place.
Regarding the reduction of dislocation of the Humerus
Know that the Humerus does not dislocate except in three ways.
The first is that it dislocates to the part of the elbow downwards Secondly, it is necessary to displace [the bone] to the part of the chest. Or perhaps to the upper part of the humerus: and that is rare
It does not displace to the back, because of the swelling. It does not displace to the front, because of the nerves
Mostly, however, it is displaced and comes down to the lower part of the chest: especially in those where the flesh is scanty Since it comes out quickly and enters quickly
In those where the flesh is plentiful, it is the opposite, that is to say because it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty
And perhaps it happens to some people by a blow or a fall, and the humerus is displaced by the swelling, and it is thought because of this that it is displaced
It is necessary therefore to examine it until you are certain of its displacement, and then you can proceed with its treatment.
It is recognized as displaced when it comes down to the lower part of the chest: and you must distinguish between a displaced humerus and a healthy humerus For indeed you will find among them a diverse manifestation, and you will find in the head of the humerus a concavity, and under the thumb there is a swelling as though there were two heads: and you cannot reduce the hand to its natural position, nor move it in any way
Similarly, if it is displaced towards the chest or upwards: then you will find a noticeable bulge, and it is not hidden
And of this displacement it is easy to reduce when it is recent, or when it is mild
And its reduction is such that it raises the minister's hand to the upper parts. And then place your fingers under the nail of that [affected] limb, and elevate the joint with force upwards to its place: and the minister will raise his hand and extend it upwards. And then support it below, for it will quickly return to its place.
If, however, it does not return by that which we have said, and if there has been a displacement for several days: then it is necessary to bathe the patient in warm water, and to apply an embolus which will soften and soothe, like decocting wild herbs, barley, and meadowsweet in water, and to administer these things
And then the patient is placed upon his back: and under the nail of that limb a sphere and a bandage are placed, which should be equal between leniency and tension.
And then the physician places his heel upon the sphere, and elevates the arm with force, and pulls the patient's hand downwards.
And if necessary, reduce it according to this method: and it is that you make a person stand by the patient who is longer than him, and that he stands at the side, and that he inserts his arm under the patient's nail: and that he elevates the nail upwards until the patient is suspended in the air And another minister pulls the patient's hand downwards into his belly If, however, the patient is limp: then it is necessary to suspend it with something else, so as to make it flexible: for it will return to its place immediately.
And it is also reduced in another way: thus it forms in the earth a long stick whose head is rounded like a glove of a hanged man: neither gross nor subtle. And then it is placed under the lower part after the upper part of the stick is covered with soft cloth: and the lower part is fixed according to the length of the stick
And then the hand is extended downwards from the other side, and the body is extended from the other side with force: for the suture returns to its place with speed.
If its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then take care of it thus: take a stick whose length is equal to the distance of two arms, and whose width is equal to the distance of four fingers, and whose thickness is equal to the distance of two fingers: whose head is rounded, so that it may fill the entrance to the depth of the wound, according to this form
Then it is bound over the rounded head with soft cloth, so that it does not harm the stick of the patient.
And it is placed under the wound of the patient, so that the hand or arm is extended over the stick to the lower part: and the stick is bound over the support and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
And the arm is placed over the step of the ladder for the width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is let go from the other side. The suture enters immediately.
When it is thus completed the entrance of it is completed according to whatever way is possible: then it is necessary that you place under the wound a sphere equal in quantity to that of wool And then put on a prepared plaster from pounded barley with oil, and white of egg over the whole arm, as it is turned upwards
Then tighten under the thumb the stricture of the decennium: and the ligament will be reversed over the base of the palm: and its hand is connected to its column and let go, neither move its hand for seven days.
It is necessary to put nourishing food to the patient until the limb be comfortable: for it is quicker for its healing.
Then put on a wider dressing after seven days, or five, and take it off when moved. If, therefore, it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
If, however, the suture is displaced many times, because of the moisture that happens to it, or for some other cause: then it is necessary to administer in it cautery with three holes, as it is stated in the chapter on cautery
If you do all this, and the ligament is dissolved after seven days, and the suture is not firm, and you reduce the palm and the stricture above it somewhat, and it does not harden, and it falls off, and it is not able to raise itself to the upper parts: then you know that the nerves that are at the head of the humerus are either cut, or extended, or softened: and you know that the suture will never be firm in its place.
As for the dislocation which happens towards the breast and the nipple, or to the back, it is done by impulse and extension with the hands: until it returns, and cautery is administered in the rest of the stricture and care, until it is healed.
If after the healing there remains greatness in the limb, and tardiness in its motion: then the patient is treated with many baths, until the greatness is softened, and it returns to its first nature.
On the treatment of dislocation of the Cubitus.
You know that the cubitus joint is easily dislocated and returns with difficulty: and that it is also prone to dislocation in all parts, and above all to the front or to the back
Its dislocation is not hidden: for it hangs loose under the skin and under the touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated And when you join the dislocated cubitus with the sound limb, it becomes evident. It is joined together again, and the arm cannot be doubled over, nor can it be touched with the other arm
And you must, in order to be expedient, reduce the dislocation immediately before a callous swelling forms on it
For if a callous swelling forms on it, its reduction becomes difficult: and it may not heal at all, especially when it is at the back For every kind of dislocation is harmful, and causes great pain: and it descends with great speed with it.
And the restoration of it when it is possible is to extend the limb with two hands: and the arm should be spread out, and the hands of the physician should be placed above the elbow and below it, and he should push with his thumbs together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place
Or if the dislocation is to the front: then it returns in such a way that the hand is doubled with the other hand, until it comes into contact with the root of the palm of the dislocated cubitus
Which if it does not yield to reduction, then administer violent extension, with force: and extend the arm with two attendants: and let them hold it firmly even, so that it does not move during the extension
Then the arm is returned to its entire extent after it has been bent over the hand, and a doubled long band or a wide ligament is passed over it.
And when the doctor grasps the hand with his grasp, let him moisten it with oil: so that this be from the things which aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he drives the joint forward with a pushing movement until it returns
And after its reduction, it is necessary to place upon it a dressing, in which there is styptic action, and drying with alum or white of egg: and the structure is to be tied down appropriately. And the arm is suspended from the collar of the patient: and it is left for a few days.
Then it is healed. If, however, the joint remains fixed in its place, then solve the ligament from it and leave it alone
And if you have not properly tied down the joint, then repeat the dressing and the ligament and leave it alone for a few days until it is properly tied down Then it is healed.
If, however, there is still some degree of pain after the reduction of the joint, and the position is painful: then administer moisture in a bath, and gentle friction, and compression, until the pain subsides, or put a poultice over the joint, that is, a piece of fat from a sheep's tail Then it is bound, and it is left for a day and a night Then take away the dressing, and put it into a bath. When it is wet, then gently friction the joint. Then repeat above the same limb twice, and the third time with the bath inside until it is softened.
And if desired, put above the same limb a cushion of fat, moistened, warmed with butter, and bind over it.</start_of_turn>

Of the treatment of a dislocated Anchae.
You know that the ligatures of the Anchae, and of the humerus, do not happen except for a complete dislocation: neither does it happen except for a slight displacement and a concavity.
And the dislocation of the Anchae is done according to four parts. That is, because it is dislocated forward and backward
Many are displaced inwards: few towards the front, and few towards the back.
The treatment of such displacement inwards is: because when you join the limb of the sick with that of the healthy, the latter is longer: and the knee of the sick is more prominent than that of the healthy, and the sick man cannot double his foot at the ankle: and the place which follows the ankle after the heel has gone is that where the head of the thigh comes.
The sign of such displacement outwards is that there are injuries contrary to these injuries
The sign of such displacement towards the front is that it expands the healthy limb along its course: but indeed it does not double itself without pain at the knee: and if he attempts to walk, he cannot go beyond that without pain: and he retains his urine and his heel is swollen, and there is a swelling at walking on the ankle
The sign of such displacement towards the back is that it does not expand the knee, and that he cannot double it before he doubles the ankle: and his limb is even shorter than the other: and his ankle is softened, and the head of the thigh is at the place of the hip bone.
The reduction of this kind of displacement is that you should join together again what has been separated for a long time, which has become callous with age. Then if you have administered the reduction, and it remains in its proper position: then there is no longer any ailment in that matter It is not right therefore that an imputation should be made to him of malice aforethought.
But in that case where the displacement is recent, and is of one of the four kinds of displacement: then it is proper in this case to wish to join together, and to extend it inwards and outwards, and to move it to the right and to the left: perhaps then it will return, and no other treatment will be needed.
If he does not return in this way, it is necessary that you prepare a strong minister and extend his leg from the lower part: either with his hands or with a ligament which is bound above his knee. And another minister extends his hand above, so that he may insert his hand under his toes. Then tighten with a soft ligament above the root of the thigh and keep the end of the other ligament taut.
And extend it either from the anterior part of the groin to the part of the hip bone, or from the posterior part to the part of the back
And let the extension be all at once, until the patient rises with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this is a common species of extension of four species.
If he therefore returns dislocated as we have said, that is good: and if not, then it is necessary to use all these methods which we have mentioned for treatment.
In reduction, however, of itself when the dislocation is towards the inside, it is necessary that the patient be placed over his sound side. Then put a ligament above the root of the thigh, on the part between the head of the thigh and the groin. Then extend the ligament upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the hip bone
Then take another strong minister of his arm and press the lower part of the thigh and extend outwards the extension which we mentioned before And thus it returns to its place
And this is the easier method of all those methods for reducing this limb.
If, however, he excuses himself from thee, and doth not obey thy order to enter with this kind of treatment: then it is necessary, that two feet of the sick man be bound together with a strong, soft ligament, over two calcanei, and over two knees: and let the length of each be as much as four fingers; and let the diseased limb be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then the diseased limb is suspended upon the head of the ligament, which is in the groin: and let its length be as much as the quantity of two arms from the ground
Then command the strong youth to bind the hip: and suspend the limb with the youth: and the youth shall push with force, bending the hip with force, for the bandage will return to its place with speed.
But in its return, when there is displacement outwards, it is necessary that the sick man lie upon a board, as we have said in the case of gibbosity. And bind the ligament over his thigh, the diseased limb itself, and over his breast.
Then two boards are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then is placed a board added in the middle of the foot, fixed well: upon which are bound wet cloths, so that it does not hurt the sick man: and let the board be between his thighs, so that it is not drawn downwards with extension.
Then let all the attendants go to their places, and the physician will equal the reduction by his hand.
If therefore he obeys the reduction, it will be good.
And if not, place upon his own table, and press it down, as we said in the case of gibbosity, equally. The patient must lie with the affected side upwards.
When the dislocation is forward, then it is necessary to extend the limb quickly: and it is in accordance with this that the physician places his right palm on the heel of the patient Then he grasps it with the other hand, and when he has closed it, he extends the limb downwards to the knee
And if the dislocation is backwards: then it is not necessary to extend the limb downwards, but rather to elevate it above the ground: indeed it is necessary to place it on a firm support, as it is also done when the dislocation is wide outwards, as we said concerning its recumbent position on two cushions: and it is upon its face and the stretched ligament: as we said above.
And it is necessary to apply a compress also with a board upon the place from which the joint has been displaced, when the reduction is complete, as is necessary
And the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend both legs of the patient: and when you remove them equal, and the patient tightens his limb and expands it without groaning: then you know that the limb has returned, as is necessary: then join together the two thighs, and apply a bandage, and bind as before, that is, with a rotation: so that it does not move outwards to any part: and the patient must rest quietly for three days, or four.
Then loosen the ligament and the bandage, and compare the limb with the other: then if you find them equal in position, then you know that the dislocation has been definitively reduced: absolutely therefore you may allow the patient to walk.
And if you see any softening: then rest, and reapply the bandage, and bind it as before: and leave it for three days.
Then heal it and make it tardy in its walking over both sides for a few days until it is comfortable.

On the kinds of dislocations with wound or fracture.
When anything of this kind happens, and you desire its cure and restoration, many things follow in its wake: and because of this it is not right to anticipate over its cure, which is of this kind, except for one who is prudent in the art of long-lasting remedies, merciful, pious, tardy, not audacious, neither presumptuous: and as the physicians sit in the aftermath of a hot bath, they do not depart without giving an order concerning the patient. Except that you know or hope for salvation from perdition with the gentleness of adversity, and in which something of hope appears To attempt to reduce it immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a cold abscess forms
If the limb returns, as we wish, then manage the abscess: and care for the wound with the things that suit it from the desiccant unguents.
If the dislocation is with fracture, and they are separated from each other in the bone, then attempt to remove them, and do in this what we said in simple ailments concerning the places where they are located
And be cautious according to your ability, and protect yourself from the entrance of fear: according to what has been predicted to you by me
For better is your prayer to your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more safe for your body in your time than all the physicians of the world
